We can compute the circumcenter O of triangle B, t_E u, t_F u. As before, from |O-B|^2 = |O - t_E u|^2, we get:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (1)

From |O-B|^2 = |O - t_F u|^2, we get:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (2)

Thus subtracting gives t_E^2 = t_F^2, so t_E = t_F (since positive). But we have t_E ≠ t_F in general. So there is a contradiction. Wait, this suggests that our assumption that O is the circumcenter of B, t_E u, t_F u is inconsistent? But that's impossible. Let's check: The points are B, t_E u, t_F u. Are they non-collinear? Yes, because B is not on the line through origin in direction u generally. So there should be a unique circumcenter. However, from the equations we have, we derived that both (1) and (2) must hold for the same O. But they both give the same left-hand side (2 O·u - 2 O·B). So the right-hand sides must be equal, giving t_E^2 = t_F^2. So this would imply t_E = t_F. But we know t_E ≠ t_F. So there must be an error: In the equation |O - t_E u|^2 = |O - B|^2, we have:

|O|^2 - 2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2 => -2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = -2 O·B + |B|^2 => 2 O·B - 2 t_E O·u = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2. So that is different from what I wrote. Let's re-derive carefully:

|O - t_E u|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 t_E (O·u) + t_E^2 |u|^2.
|O - B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2.

Set equal: |O|^2 - 2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2 => -2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = -2 O·B + |B|^2 => 2 O·B - 2 t_E O·u = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2. (Equation 1)

Similarly, for F: 2 O·B - 2 t_F O·u = |B|^2 - t_F^2 |u|^2. (Equation 2)

Now, subtract (1) and (2): -2 (t_E - t_F) O·u = - (t_E^2 - t_F^2) |u|^2 => 2 (t_E - t_F) O·u = (t_E^2 - t_F^2) |u|^2 = (t_E - t_F)(t_E + t_F) |u|^2 => if t_E≠t_F, then O·u = ((t_E + t_F)/2) |u|^2. So that's fine. Then from (1), we get:

2 O·B = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2 + 2 t_E O·u = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2 + 2 t_E * ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2 = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2 + t_E(t_E+t_F) |u|^2 = |B|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2.

So O·B = (|B|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2)/2. So we have two linear conditions: O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2 and O·B = (|B|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2)/2. So O is determined.

Thus there is no contradiction; the two equations are consistent. So we have:

(1) O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2.
(2) O·B = (|B|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2)/2.

And p_x + r - x0 = (1+R-r)/2 + r - x0 = (1+R+r)/2 - x0. But x0 = (1+r^2-R^2)/2, so p_x + r - x0 = (1+R+r - (1+r^2-R^2))/2 = (R+r - r^2 + R^2)/2 = (R^2 - r^2 + R+r)/2 = (R-r)(R+r+1)/2 = v_x. So (x0+r)p_x + y0^2 = (r+x0) v_x. Thus v_y = - (r+x0) v_x / y0. So v = (v_x, - (r+x0) v_x / y0). So v is proportional to (1, - (r+x0)/y0). So v is collinear with the vector (1, - (r+x0)/y0). That is interesting.

Thus u = v = (v_x, v_y) = v_x (1, - (r+x0)/y0). So the direction of AP is determined by the vector (1, - (r+x0)/y0). So the line AP has slope = - (r+x0)/y0.

Now, note that the line through M and P? Not.

Now, we have H = (p_x, p_x(1-p_x)/p_y). We can also express p_y in terms of v_x, etc. Since v_x = (R-r)(R+r+1)/2, and p_x = (1+R-r)/2, p_y = - (x0+r) p_x / y0. So H_x = p_x, H_y = p_x(1-p_x)/p_y = p_x(1-p_x) / ( - (x0+r) p_x / y0 ) = - y0 (1-p_x)/(x0+r). So H = (p_x, - y0 (1-p_x)/(x0+r)). Good.

Now, the line L_H: through H with direction v = v_x (1, - (r+x0)/y0). So its equation: (x,y) = (p_x, - y0 (1-p_x)/(x0+r)) + t v_x (1, - (r+x0)/y0). Since v_x is a scalar, we can absorb it into t. So we can write L_H: (x,y) = (p_x, - y0 (1-p_x)/(x0+r)) + s (1, - (r+x0)/y0). So the direction vector is w = (1, - (r+x0)/y0). So L_H is the line through H with slope - (r+x0)/y0.

Now, points: B = (x0, -y0). E = A + t_E v, with t_E = -2 A·v/|v|^2. But note that v is proportional to w, so v = v_x w. So A·v = v_x (A·w). Thus t_E = -2 v_x (A·w)/|v|^2. But |v|^2 = v_x^2 |w|^2. So t_E = -2 (A·w)/|w|^2. So t_E is the scalar such that E = A + t_E v = A + t_E v_x w = A + (t_E v_x) w. And we have t_E v_x = -2 (A·w)/|w|^2 * v_x? Wait, t_E = -2 (A·w)/|w|^2, so t_E v_x = -2 v_x (A·w)/|w|^2. So E = A + λ w with λ = -2 v_x (A·w)/|w|^2. But that's messy. However, note that we also have t_F = -2 (A-n)·v/|v|^2 = -2 v_x (A-n)·w / |w|^2. So t_F v_x = -2 v_x (A-n)·w/|w|^2. So F = A + μ w with μ = -2 v_x (A-n)·w/|w|^2.

Thus E and F are on the line through A with direction w (since w is direction of AP). So they are points on the line AP, which we already know. So the circle (BEF) is the circle through B, A+λ w, A+μ w, where λ and μ are real numbers.

Now, we want to show that line through H with direction w is tangent to circle (BEF). That is, the line L_H: (x,y) = H + s w is tangent to circumcircle of triangle B, A+λ w, A+μ w.

Now, note that H = (p_x, - y0 (1-p_x)/(x0+r)). And we have w = (1, - (r+x0)/y0). So H is on the line through? Possibly H is the midpoint of something? Let's compute H in terms of A and the x-axis. Since p_x = (1+R-r)/2, and R and r are related to A.

Given the time, I think we should attempt to compute O using the formula for circumcenter of B, A+λ w, A+μ w. Since these three points are not collinear, we can compute O as intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of B and A+λ w is line through midpoint of B and A+λ w, with direction perpendicular to (A+λ w - B). That might be messy. However, we can use the vector approach: Let’s denote:

- b = B = (x0, -y0)
- a = A = (x0, y0)
- w = (1, - (r+x0)/y0)
- λ = t_E v_x? Actually, we have E = A + t_E v, but v = v_x w, so E = A + t_E v_x w. Let’s define α = t_E v_x, β = t_F v_x. Then E = A + α w, F = A + β w.

Now, we need to find O such that |O-b|^2 = |O - A - α w|^2 = |O - A - β w|^2.

From |O-b|^2 = |O - A - α w|^2, we get:

2 O·b - 2 O·(A+α w) = |b|^2 - |A+α w|^2 => 2 O·(b - A - α w) = |b|^2 - |A+α w|^2. (1)

Similarly, from |O-b|^2 = |O - A - β w|^2, we get:

2 O·(b - A - β w) = |b|^2 - |A+β w|^2. (2)

Subtract (1) and (2): 2 O·( (b - A - α w) - (b - A - β w) ) = (|b|^2 - |A+α w|^2) - (|b|^2 - |A+β w|^2) => 2 O·( (β - α) w ) = |A+β w|^2 - |A+α w|^2 => 2 (β-α) O·w = (|A+β w|^2 - |A+α w|^2). But |A+β w|^2 - |A+α w|^2 = (β^2 - α^2) |w|^2 + 2(β-α) A·w = (β-α)(β+α)|w|^2 + 2(β-α) A·w = (β-α)( (β+α)|w|^2 + 2 A·w ). So if β≠α, we get:

2 O·w = (β+α)|w|^2 + 2 A·w. (3)

Thus O·w = ((β+α)/2) |w|^2 + A·w.

Now, from (1): 2 O·(b - A - α w) = |b|^2 - |A+α w|^2. Expand RHS: |b|^2 - (|A|^2 + 2α A·w + α^2 |w|^2) = |b|^2 - r^2 - 2α A·w - α^2 |w|^2. But |b|^2 = x0^2 + y0^2 = r^2. So RHS = -2α A·w - α^2 |w|^2. So (1) becomes:

2 O·(b - A - α w) = -2α A·w - α^2 |w|^2 => divide 2: O·(b - A - α w) = -α A·w - (α^2/2)|w|^2. (1')

Similarly, we could also use (2) to get O·(b - A - β w) = -β A·w - (β^2/2)|w|^2.

Now, we also have O·w from (3). And we can express O·b and O·A in terms of O·w and other conditions? Since b and A are symmetric: b = (x0,-y0), A = (x0,y0). So b - A = (0,-2y0). And b + A = (2x0,0). So b = (x0, -y0) = A - 2y0 e2. Not directly.

We can write O = O_parallel + O_perp. But maybe we can find O explicitly using the linear system. Since we have O·w = something, and O·(b - A - α w) = something, we can combine to get O·b and O·A. Let’s compute O·b and O·A in terms of O·w and O·(b-A) = O·(0,-2y0) = -2y0 O_y. Not helpful.

Given the time, I think we should consider that the algebraic verification, while messy, is doable. Since we have a specific coordinate system with d=1, we can compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF using the formula for circumcenter of three points. There is a formula: For points (x1,y1), (x2,y2), (x3,y3), the circumcenter is given by:

X = ( (x1^2+y1^2)(y2-y3) + (x2^2+y2^2)(y3-y1) + (x3^2+y3^2)(y1-y2) ) / ( 2( x1(y2-y3) + x2(y3-y1) + x3(y1-y2) ) )
Y = ( (x1^2+y1^2)(x3-x2) + (x2^2+y2^2)(x1-x3) + (x3^2+y3^2)(x2-x1) ) / ( 2( x1(y2-y3) + x2(y3-y1) + x3(y1-y2) ) )

This is a known formula. So we can compute O using this formula for points B, E, F. Then compute the distance from O to line L_H. Since the line L_H has equation in the form y = m x + c, where m = - (r+x0)/y0. Then the distance from O to that line is |m X_O - Y_O + c| / sqrt(m^2+1). And we need to show that this distance equals the radius R_BEF = distance from O to B. That is, |m X_O - Y_O + c| = sqrt(m^2+1) * sqrt( (X_O - x0)^2 + Y_O^2 ). Squaring both sides gives an identity. This identity can be verified by substituting the expressions for X_O, Y_O, m, c in terms of x0, y0, r, R, etc. Since it's a lot of algebra, we can try to simplify using the relations between x0,y0,r,R. The key is to show that the condition holds.

Given the time constraints, I think it's acceptable to present a computational proof that is rigorous but may be lengthy. However, the problem likely expects a synthetic solution. Since we are to output a proof, we can present a clean analytic proof.

(Equation 2)

In triangle PMN, the Simson line of a point X on its circumcircle is the line containing the feet of perpendiculars from X to the sides. For triangle PMN, the circumcircle is the circle through P, M, N. Is A on that circle? In general, no. So not.

First, compute the circle by solving the system: (X - x_i)^2 + (Y - y_i)^2 = R^2.

Subtract B and E:

x_B^2+y_B^2 = 0.1296+0.2304=0.36.
x_E^2+y_E^2 = 0.2304+0.1296=0.36. So LHS=0. So 0 = 2(0.36-0.48)X + 2(-0.48+0.36)Y = 2(-0.12)X + 2(-0.12)Y = -0.24X -0.24Y => X+Y=0. So first condition: X+Y=0.

So equation: -0.32 = -0.32X + 0.32Y => Divide by 0.32: -1 = -X + Y => Y = X -1. So second condition: Y = X -1.

Now, we can try to compute O in general using the formulas for B, E, F. We have:

- B = (x0, -y0)
- A = (x0, y0)
- v = P - A = (p_x - x0, p_y - y0) where P is circumcenter of ACD.

We can compute t_E = -2 A·v/|v|^2, t_F = -2 (A-N)·v/|v|^2.

Then E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

We want to find O such that |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2.

We can try to derive O using the vector approach. Let’s denote:

u = v. Then E = A + α u, F = A + β u, where α = t_E, β = t_F.

We have B, A, u.

We want to find O such that (O-B)^2 = (O-A-α u)^2 = (O-A-β u)^2.

From the last two, we get:

|O-A-α u|^2 = |O-A-β u|^2 => (O-A)^2 - 2α (O-A)·u + α^2|u|^2 = (O-A)^2 - 2β (O-A)·u + β^2|u|^2 => -2α (O-A)·u + α^2|u|^2 = -2β (O-A)·u + β^2|u|^2 => 2(β-α)(O-A)·u = (β^2-α^2)|u|^2 => (β-α)[2(O-A)·u - (β+α)|u|^2] = 0 => if β≠α, then (O-A)·u = ((β+α)/2)|u|^2. (Equation 1)

Similarly, from |O-B|^2 = |O-A-α u|^2, we get:

|O|^2 - 2O·B + |B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2(O·(A+α u)) + |A+α u|^2 => -2O·B + |B|^2 = -2O·A -2α O·u + |A|^2 + 2α A·u + α^2|u|^2. So 2O·(A-B) = |A+α u|^2 - |B|^2 + 2α A·u? M is on line MN, but not necessarily on the circle. Possibly we can find that P lies on the perpendicular bisector of AB? Since A and B are symmetric across x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of AB is x-axis (MN). Actually AB is vertical line x=x0, so its perpendicular bisector is horizontal line through its midpoint (x0,0). That's y=0, which is MN. So MN is the perpendicular bisector of AB. So M and N lie on that line. That might be useful.

Now, consider the circle (BEF). Since B is reflection of A across MN, and E and F are on line through A with direction v, maybe the circle (BEF) is symmetric to something. Also note that the line through H parallel to AP is parallel to EF. So we need to show that this line is tangent to circle (BEF). Possibly we can show that the distance from the center of (BEF) to this line equals the radius, which is the same as the distance from the center to the line EF? Actually, for a circle, the distance from the center to a chord is less than the radius, and the tangent parallel to the chord is at distance equal to the radius from the center, on the opposite side of the chord? More precisely, if a line is parallel to a chord, it is tangent if and only if the distance from the center to that line equals the radius. And the distance from the center to the chord is the perpendicular distance. So if we can show that the line through H is at a distance equal to the radius from the center, and that the chord EF is at some distance d_c from the center, then the line through H is the line parallel to EF at distance radius from center. But we need to prove that the line through H is exactly that line. That is, H is such that the line through H parallel to EF is tangent. So H must lie on a line parallel to EF at distance radius from center. But we need to prove that H lies on that specific line. So maybe we can compute the distance from H to the center of (BEF) along the direction perpendicular to EF, and show that it equals the radius? Actually, if the line through H is parallel to EF, then the distance from H to the center is not directly the radius; the line's distance from the center is the perpendicular distance. So we need to find the perpendicular distance from the center to the line through H parallel to EF. That distance equals the distance from the center to H projected onto the perpendicular direction. So we need to show that the absolute value of the component of (center - H) perpendicular to v equals the radius. That is, |(center - H) × v| / |v| = R_circ, where R_circ is the circumradius of BEF.

Alternatively, we can show that the power of H with respect to circle (BEF) is equal to the square of the distance from H to the point of tangency? Not helpful.

Maybe we can find a transformation: Since H is orthocenter of PMN, and AP is the line through A and P, maybe there is a circle that is the image of the circumcircle of PMN under some homothety. Or perhaps the line through H parallel to AP is the Simson line of point A with respect to triangle PMN? Actually, for a point on the circumcircle of triangle PMN, the Simson line is the line containing the feet of perpendiculars to the sides. But A is not necessarily on circumcircle of PMN. However, H is orthocenter, and there is a known relation: The reflection of H across a side lies on the circumcircle. Not sure.

Let's attempt analytic computation; it might be long but doable. We can simplify by using coordinates and maybe eliminating some variables.

Set up:

M=(0,0), N=(d,0), A=(x0,y0), B=(x0,-y0). Let r = radius of Ω, R = radius of Γ.

We have x0 = (d^2 + r^2 - R^2)/(2d). Also y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2.

Let’s denote:

p_x = (d+R-r)/2. (1)

We need p_y. From earlier:

p_y = (1/2)(y0 - ((x0+r)(d+R-x0))/y0). So:

p_y = (1/2)(y0 - (x0+r)(d+R-x0)/y0). (2)

Now, v = (p_x - x0, p_y - y0). Let's compute these components:

Δx = p_x - x0 = (d+R-r)/2 - x0 = (d+R-r - 2x0)/2.
Δy = p_y - y0 = (1/2)(y0 - (x0+r)(d+R-x0)/y0) - y0 = (1/2)(y0 - (x0+r)(d+R-x0)/y0 - 2y0) = (1/2)( -y0 - (x0+r)(d+R-x0)/y0 ) = -(1/2)( y0 + (x0+r)(d+R-x0)/y0 ).

So Δy = - (1/2)( y0 + (x0+r)(d+R-x0)/y0 ).

Now, |v|^2 = Δx^2 + Δy^2.

Now, compute t_E = -2(x0 Δx + y0 Δy)/|v|^2. But note that x0 Δx + y0 Δy = x0(p_x - x0) + y0(p_y - y0) = x0 p_x - x0^2 + y0 p_y - y0^2 = x0 p_x + y0 p_y - (x0^2+y0^2) = x0 p_x + y0 p_y - r^2.

So t_E = -2( x0 p_x + y0 p_y - r^2 ) / |v|^2.

Similarly, t_F = 2( (d-x0)Δx - y0Δy )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)(p_x-x0) - y0(p_y-y0) )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - (d-x0)x0 - y0 p_y + y0^2 )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + x0^2 - y0 p_y + y0^2 )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + (x0^2+y0^2) - y0 p_y )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + r^2 - y0 p_y )/|v|^2.

Now, note that we can also compute (d-x0) and p_x etc. We'll need to simplify these expressions using relations.

Maybe we can find that t_E and t_F have some simple forms. For instance, perhaps t_E = -2? Or something like that? Let's test with a specific numeric example to guess patterns. Choose simple numbers: Let d=5, r=3, R=4. Then x0 = (25+9-16)/(10)=18/10=1.8. y0^2 = 9 - 3.24 = 5.76, y0=2.4. So A=(1.8,2.4), B=(1.8,-2.4). C=(-3,0), D=(5+4,0)=9,0? Actually d+R=5+4=9, so D=(9,0). Then p_x = (d+R-r)/2 = (5+4-3)/2=6/2=3. So p_x=3. Now p_y = (1/2)(y0 - ((x0+r)(d+R-x0))/y0) = (1/2)(2.4 - ((1.8+3)(5+4-1.8))/2.4) = (1/2)(2.4 - (4.8*(7.2))/2.4) = (1/2)(2.4 - (34.56)/2.4) = (1/2)(2.4 - 14.4) = (1/2)(-12) = -6. So P=(3,-6). So v = P-A = (3-1.8, -6-2.4) = (1.2, -8.4). So slope = -8.4/1.2 = -7. So AP line slope -7.

Now, compute H: orthocenter of triangle PMN: P(3,-6), M(0,0), N(5,0). Since MN horizontal, altitude from P is vertical line x=3. So H x=3. Altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. PN: from P to N: (5-3, 0-(-6)) = (2,6). So direction (2,6). A perpendicular vector is (6,-2) or (3,-1). So altitude from M: line through origin with direction (3,-1) or (3,-1) gives slope -1/3. Equation y = (-1/3)x. Intersection with x=3 gives y = -1. So H=(3,-1). Good.

Now, line through H parallel to AP: slope -7, through (3,-1): equation y+1 = -7(x-3) => y = -7x+21-1 = -7x+20.

Now, find E and F: E = second intersection of line AP with Ω (circle centered at M radius 3). Line AP: through A(1.8,2.4) and P(3,-6). Param: A + t v = (1.8+1.2t, 2.4-8.4t). For t=1 we get P. For t=0 we get A. We need t such that point lies on circle x^2+y^2=9. Solve (1.8+1.2t)^2 + (2.4-8.4t)^2 = 9. Expand: (1.8^2=3.24, 2*1.8*1.2=4.32, (1.2)^2=1.44) so first term: 3.24+4.32t+1.44t^2. Second: (2.4^2=5.76, 2*2.4*(-8.4)= -40.32, (8.4)^2=70.56) so: 5.76 -40.32t +70.56t^2. Sum: (3.24+5.76)=9, t terms: 4.32-40.32 = -36, t^2: 1.44+70.56=72. So equation: 9 -36t +72t^2 = 9 => -36t+72t^2=0 => t(72t-36)=0 => t=0 or t=0.5. So t_E=0.5. Then E = (1.8+1.2*0.5, 2.4-8.4*0.5) = (1.8+0.6, 2.4-4.2) = (2.4, -1.8). So E=(2.4, -1.8). Good.

Now, F = second intersection of line AP with Γ (center N=(5,0) radius 4). Circle: (x-5)^2+y^2=16. Param: same line. Compute ( (1.8+1.2t)-5 )^2 + (2.4-8.4t)^2 = (1.8+1.2t-5)^2 + (2.4-8.4t)^2 = (-3.2+1.2t)^2 + (2.4-8.4t)^2 = (1.2t-3.2)^2 + (2.4-8.4t)^2. Expand: (1.44t^2 - 2*1.2*3.2 t + 10.24) = 1.44t^2 -7.68t+10.24. Second: (8.4^2=70.56t^2 -2*2.4*8.4 t + 5.76) = 70.56t^2 -40.32t+5.76. Sum: 72t^2 -48t+16 = 16? Actually 10.24+5.76=16. So equation: 72t^2 -48t +16 = 16 => 72t^2 -48t =0 => t(72t-48)=0 => t=0 or t=2/3? Actually 48/72=2/3, so t_F=2/3. Then F = (1.8+1.2*(2/3), 2.4-8.4*(2/3)) = (1.8+0.8, 2.4-5.6) = (2.6, -3.2). So F=(2.6,-3.2). Good.

Now, triangle BEF: B=(1.8,-2.4), E=(2.4,-1.8), F=(2.6,-3.2). We want to show that line through H parallel to AP (i.e., line y = -7x+20) is tangent to circumcircle of BEF. Compute circumcircle of BEF. Let's find its equation. We can compute center and radius.

Points: B(1.8, -2.4), E(2.4, -1.8), F(2.6, -3.2). Compute perpendicular bisectors.

First, midpoint of BE: ((1.8+2.4)/2=2.1, (-2.4-1.8)/2 = -2.1). So M_BE=(2.1,-2.1). Slope of BE: ( -1.8+2.4)/(2.4-1.8) = (0.6)/(0.6)=1. So perpendicular slope = -1. So line: y+2.1 = -1(x-2.1) => y = -x+2.1-2.1 = -x. So line: y = -x.

Second, midpoint of BF: ((1.8+2.6)/2=2.2, (-2.4-3.2)/2 = -2.8). So M_BF=(2.2,-2.8). Slope of BF: (-3.2+2.4)/(2.6-1.8)= (-0.8)/(0.8)=-1. So perpendicular slope = 1. Line: y+2.8 = 1(x-2.2) => y = x -2.2 -2.8 = x -5. So line: y = x -5.

Intersection of these two lines: set -x = x-5 => 2x=5 => x=2.5, y=-2.5. So center O_BEF = (2.5, -2.5). Radius: distance from O to B: (2.5-1.8=0.7, -2.5+2.4=-0.1) => sqrt(0.49+0.01)= sqrt(0.5)= sqrt(1/2)= √0.5 ≈0.7071. So circumradius = √0.5.

Now, line through H parallel to AP: y = -7x+20. Distance from center (2.5,-2.5) to this line: line in standard form: 7x + y -20 = 0? Actually y = -7x+20 => 7x+y-20=0. Distance = |7*2.5 + (-2.5) -20| / sqrt(7^2+1^2) = |17.5 -2.5 -20| / sqrt(50) = | -5| / (5√2) = 5/(5√2)=1/√2 = √0.5. That equals the radius! So indeed the line is tangent. Good.

Thus in this numeric example, the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). The distance from center to line equals radius.

Now, we need to prove this generally. The analytic approach: compute O_BEF and the distance from O_BEF to line L_H, and show that it equals the circumradius of BEF. That seems plausible but messy. However, we can try to find a synthetic reasoning.

Notice that in the numeric example, the center O_BEF = (2.5,-2.5) which is exactly the midpoint of the segment connecting A and something? Actually A=(1.8,2.4), B=(1.8,-2.4). The midpoint of AB is (1.8,0). Not that. O_BEF = (2.5,-2.5) is also the reflection of P? P=(3,-6). Not that. But note that O_BEF = (d/2, -y0)? Actually d=5, y0=2.4, so d/2=2.5, -y0=-2.4, not -2.5. So not exactly. However, the line through H parallel to AP: H=(3,-1), line slope -7. The distance from O_BEF to that line equals radius. Perhaps there is a relation: O_BEF is the midpoint of the segment connecting the projections? Not sure.

Let's try to compute general expressions.

We have:

M=(0,0), N=(d,0), A=(x0,y0), B=(x0,-y0).

Define p_x = (d+R-r)/2. And p_y as given.

We have v = (Δx, Δy) = (p_x - x0, p_y - y0).

We have t_E = -2(x0 Δx + y0 Δy)/|v|^2 = -2( x0 p_x + y0 p_y - r^2 ) / |v|^2.

t_F = 2( (d-x0)Δx - y0Δy )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + r^2 - y0 p_y )/|v|^2? Let's derive properly.

We had: t_F = 2( (d-x0)(p_x-x0) - y0(p_y-y0) )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - (d-x0)x0 - y0 p_y + y0^2 )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + x0^2 - y0 p_y + y0^2 )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + (x0^2+y0^2) - y0 p_y )/|v|^2 = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + r^2 - y0 p_y )/|v|^2.

So t_F = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + r^2 - y0 p_y )/|v|^2.

Now, we can also compute t_E and t_F in terms of something like the power of P with respect to circles? Let's compute A·(P-A) = x0Δx+y0Δy = x0p_x+y0p_y - r^2. So t_E = -2 (x0p_x+y0p_y - r^2)/|v|^2.

Similarly, (d-x0)Δx - y0Δy = (d-x0)p_x - y0p_y - ((d-x0)x0 - y0^2) = (d-x0)p_x - y0p_y - (x0d - x0^2 - y0^2) = (d-x0)p_x - y0p_y - (x0d - r^2). So t_F = 2( (d-x0)p_x - y0p_y - x0d + r^2 )/|v|^2. That matches previous.

Now, note that we might also compute t_E and t_F using the fact that P is circumcenter of ACD. Possibly we can find that t_E = -2? Not in example; t_E=0.5. So not constant.

But maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) has some nice property: Its center lies on the line through H perpendicular to AP? In example, O_BEF = (2.5,-2.5), H=(3,-1). The vector from H to O is (-0.5,-1.5). The line AP has slope -7, so direction (1,-7). The perpendicular direction is (7,1). The vector (-0.5,-1.5) dot (7,1) = -3.5-1.5=-5, not zero. So not perpendicular.

Perhaps we can find that the line through H parallel to AP is the polar of A with respect to circle (BEF)? Let's test: In example, A=(1.8,2.4). The polar of A wrt circle (BEF) (center (2.5,-2.5), radius √0.5) would be line: (x-2.5)(x0-2.5)+(y+2.5)(y0+2.5)=r^2? Actually for circle (X-O)^2 = r^2, the polar of point P is (P-O)·(X-O)=r^2. So polar of A: (A-O)·(X-O)=r^2. Compute A-O = (1.8-2.5, 2.4+2.5)=(-0.7,4.9). So polar: (-0.7)(x-2.5)+4.9(y+2.5)=0.5? Actually r^2=0.5. So equation: -0.7x+1.75+4.9y+12.25=0.5 => -0.7x+4.9y+14=0.5 => -0.7x+4.9y+13.5=0 => multiply by 10: -7x+49y+135=0 => 7x-49y-135=0. Not parallel to AP (slope -7). So not that.

Maybe we can find that H is the orthocenter of triangle PMN, and AP is the line through A and P. There is a known configuration: The circle through B, E, F might be the circle with diameter something like the reflection of A across H? Not sure.

Alternatively, we might use the concept of the Simson line: For a point on the circumcircle of a triangle, the feet of perpendiculars to the sides are collinear. Here we have triangle PMN. The point A might be such that its Simson line with respect to triangle PMN is something like the line through H parallel to AP? The orthocenter H is the point such that the reflections of H across the sides lie on the circumcircle. Given the difficulty, perhaps the intended solution uses inversion about the circle with diameter MN or something. Let's explore.

We have two circles intersecting at A and B, with centers M and N. The line MN meets Ω at C and Γ at D, with order C, M, N, D. So M is between C and N, N between M and D. So C and D are external points relative to the segment MN. Consider the inversion with center A? Or consider the homothety that sends Ω to Γ? Since the circles intersect, there is a homothety that sends Ω to Γ at the intersection of their tangents? Actually, if two circles intersect at two points, there are two homothety centers: one internal (the intersection of lines joining corresponding points of the circles) and one external (the intersection of lines joining points that are opposite). The internal homothety center is the intersection of lines through the two points of intersection? Actually, for two circles that intersect, the homothety centers are the two points on the line of centers: one is between the centers (internal), and the other is outside the segment (external). But these are the centers of homothety that map one circle to the other. The internal homothety center is the point on MN such that the ratio of distances to the centers equals the ratio of radii. That point is the point dividing MN internally in the ratio r:R. That point is not necessarily A or B. The external homothety center divides MN externally. But these homothety centers are not A or B; they are on MN.

Maybe we can consider the circle (ACD). Its center P is the circumcenter. So P is the center of a circle through A, C, D. Since C and D are on MN, and A is on the common chord, perhaps there is a relation that AP is the radical axis of circles Ω and Γ? Actually, the radical axis of Ω and Γ is line AB. That's not AP. But maybe AP is the line through A and the center of the spiral similarity that sends C to D? Not sure.

Another approach: Use coordinate geometry but with more algebraic simplification. We can compute O_BEF and distance to line L_H. Since the problem is likely to have a synthetic solution, but we can present an analytic solution as long as we do it carefully. However, the problem says "Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." So we need to provide a rigorous proof. Using analytic geometry is acceptable, but we need to do the algebra in a systematic way, and we need to justify every step. It might be long, but we can try to simplify using vector notation and dot products.

Let's set up vectors with origin at M = (0,0). Then N = n (vector), with n = (d,0). Let a = A, b = B = reflection of A across MN (x-axis), so b = (x0, -y0). Then C = -r * u, where u is unit vector along MN? Actually MN is along x-axis, so C = (-r, 0). D = n + R * u = (d+R, 0) (since u = (1,0)). So C and D are on x-axis.

Now, P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. So P = intersection of perpendicular bisectors of CD and AC. Since CD is horizontal, its perpendicular bisector is vertical line x = (C_x + D_x)/2 = ((-r)+(d+R))/2 = (d+R-r)/2. So P has x-coordinate p_x = (d+R-r)/2. Its y-coordinate is determined by perpendicular bisector of AC.

Let’s denote the coordinates in vector form: Let e1 = (1,0), e2 = (0,1). Then M=0, N = d e1. A = (x0, y0) = x0 e1 + y0 e2. B = x0 e1 - y0 e2. C = -r e1. D = (d+R) e1.

Now, we need to compute P. The perpendicular bisector of AC: The midpoint of A and C is ( (x0 - r)/2, y0/2). The line AC has direction A - C = (x0 + r, y0). So a normal vector is (y0, -(x0+r)) (since dot: (x0+r)*y0 + y0*(-(x0+r))=0). So the perpendicular bisector equation: (X - M_AC)·(y0, -(x0+r)) = 0? Actually the line is set of points X such that (X - M_AC) is perpendicular to AC. So direction vector of line is perpendicular to AC, so the line is given by (X - M_AC) dot (A-C) = 0? Wait, for the perpendicular bisector, the line is perpendicular to AC and passes through M_AC. So its direction is perpendicular to AC. So the line equation: (X - M_AC) · (A-C) = 0? No, that would give line with normal vector A-C, which is perpendicular to the line. Actually, a line perpendicular to AC has direction perpendicular to AC, so its normal vector is parallel to AC. So the line equation: (X - M_AC) · (A-C) = 0 (since A-C is normal). Yes, that's correct: For a line perpendicular to AC, the vector from any point on the line to the midpoint is perpendicular to the line, so it's parallel to AC. So the dot product with (A-C) is zero. So the line: (X - M_AC)·(A-C)=0. That is the equation of the perpendicular bisector. So for point X = (x,y), we have (x - (x0-r)/2, y - y0/2)·(x0+r, y0) = 0. That is: (x - (x0-r)/2)(x0+r) + (y - y0/2) y0 = 0. Expand: (x0+r)x - (x0+r)(x0-r)/2 + y0 y - y0^2/2 = 0. So (x0+r)x + y0 y = (x0+r)(x0-r)/2 + y0^2/2 = (x0^2 - r^2 + y0^2)/2 = (r^2 - r^2)/2? Since x0^2+y0^2=r^2, then x0^2 - r^2 + y0^2 = 0. So right side is 0. So the equation simplifies to: (x0+r)x + y0 y = 0. That's interesting! So the perpendicular bisector of AC is the line: (x0+r)x + y0 y = 0. And we already have vertical line x = p_x = (d+R-r)/2. So P is the intersection: x = p_x, and (x0+r) p_x + y0 y_P = 0 => y_P = - (x0+r) p_x / y0. So:

p_y = - (x0+r) p_x / y0. (3)

Check with numeric: x0=1.8, r=3, p_x=3, y0=2.4 => p_y = - (4.8)*3/2.4 = -14.4/2.4 = -6, matches.

So we have a much simpler expression for p_y! That's great.

Now, p_x = (d+R-r)/2. So:

p_y = - (x0+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y0). (3')

Thus P = ( (d+R-r)/2, - (x0+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y0) ). So P is expressed nicely.

Now, we also have A = (x0, y0). So vector v = P - A = (p_x - x0, p_y - y0) = ( (d+R-r)/2 - x0, - (x0+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y0) - y0 ).

Compute Δx = p_x - x0 = (d+R-r - 2x0)/2.

Δy = - (x0+r)(d+R-r)/(2 y0) - y0 = -[ (x0+r)(d+R-r) + 2 y0^2 ]/(2 y0). But note that y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. So 2 y0^2 = 2r^2 - 2x0^2. Also (x0+r)(d+R-r) = (x0+r)(d - r + R). Not sure if simplification.

Alternatively, we can also express p_y in terms of x0, y0, d, r, R. But we might not need explicit v components; we can work with dot products.

Now, we have H = orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=0, N=n, and P is given. We already computed H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y) (since p_y ≠ 0 generally). But we can also find H in vector form: H = P + M + N - 2O, but O = circumcenter of PMN. Since M and N are on x-axis, O = (d/2, y_O). But maybe we can find H more directly: In triangle with vertices P, M, N, the orthocenter H satisfies: (H-M)·(N-P)=0? Actually, the altitude from M is perpendicular to PN, so (H-M)·(N-P)=0. Since M=0, this gives H·(N-P)=0. Similarly, altitude from N: (H-N)·(M-P)=0 => (H-N)·(M-P)=0. And altitude from P: (H-P)·(M-N)=0. So we have H·(N-P)=0 and (H-N)·(M-P)=0.

Since M=0, N=n, M-P = -P, so (H-n)·(-P)=0 => (H-n)·P=0 => H·P = n·P. And H·(N-P)=0 => H·(n-P)=0 => H·n = H·P.

Thus H·n = H·P = n·P. So H·n = n·P. Also H·P = n·P.

So we have H·n = n·P and H·P = n·P. So H·n = H·P = n·P.

Now, H lies on the altitude from P, which is perpendicular to MN (i.e., perpendicular to n). So (H-P)·n = 0 => H·n = P·n. That matches H·n = n·P. So altitude from P gives that. So H is determined by: H·n = n·P, and H·P = n·P. And also H lies in the plane (2D). So we can solve for H. In coordinates, we already have H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y). But we can also express H in terms of P and n.

Given that n = (d,0), P = (p_x, p_y). Then n·P = d p_x. So H·n = d p_x. So H_x * d = d p_x => H_x = p_x. And H·P = p_x p_x + H_y p_y = p_x^2 + H_y p_y = d p_x => H_y = (d p_x - p_x^2)/p_y = p_x(d-p_x)/p_y. So consistent.

Now, line L_H through H parallel to AP: direction v = P - A. So line: X = H + t v.

We need to show that this line is tangent to circle (BEF). So we need to prove that the circumcircle of B, E, F has exactly one intersection with L_H, or equivalently, that the distance from its center to L_H equals its radius.

We can find E and F using projection method. Since we have coordinates, we can compute E and F explicitly in terms of A, P, M, N.

Define v = P - A. Then as derived, E = A + t_E v, where t_E = -2(A·v)/|v|^2? Actually t_E = -2 (A·(P-A))/|P-A|^2 = -2 (A·v)/|v|^2. But careful: A·v = A·(P-A) = A·P - |A|^2 = A·P - r^2 (since |A|=r). So t_E = -2(A·P - r^2)/|v|^2 = 2(r^2 - A·P)/|v|^2.

Similarly, F = A + t_F v, where t_F = -2 (A - N)·v/|v|^2? Actually (A-N)·v = (A-N)·(P-A). But note that A is on Γ, so (A-N)·(A-N)=R^2. But we can also use the formula: For point X = A + t v, on Γ: |X-N|^2 = R^2. Since A is on Γ, we have |A-N|^2=R^2. Then equation: |A-N + t v|^2 = R^2 => 2t (A-N)·v + t^2|v|^2 = 0 => t(2(A-N)·v + t|v|^2)=0. So t=0 or t = -2(A-N)·v/|v|^2. So t_F = -2(A-N)·v/|v|^2. But we also derived t_F = 2( (d-x0)p_x - x0 d + r^2 - y0 p_y )/|v|^2. That's consistent.

Now, we have B = reflection of A across x-axis: B = (x0, -y0). So B = (x0, -y0). Also note that A and B are symmetric.

Maybe we can find its center and radius in terms of A, P, M, N. Since we have coordinates, we can compute O_BEF directly from points B, E, F. Let's derive general formulas.

We have:

M=0, N=n (n = (d,0)). A = a = (x0, y0). B = b = (x0, -y0). P = p = (p_x, p_y). v = p - a.

Define t_E = 2(r^2 - a·p)/|v|^2. And t_F = -2(a-n)·v/|v|^2.

Then E = a + t_E v, F = a + t_F v.

Now, we want to find circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since B, E, F are not collinear (unless degenerate). We can compute O as intersection of perpendicular bisectors of BE and BF. Because we have coordinates, we can derive O in vector form.

We already computed a·p =? Let's compute a·p = x0 p_x + y0 p_y = x0 p_x + y0 (-p_x (x0+r)/y0) = x0 p_x - p_x (x0+r) = -p_x r. So a·p = -p_x r. That's nice! Because y0 cancels. So a·p = -p_x r. Now, compute (a-n)·v. Since v = p - a, (a-n)·(p-a) = (a-n)·p - (a-n)·a = (a·p - n·p) - (a·a - n·a) = (a·p - n·p) - (r^2 - a·n). But we know a·p = -p_x r. And n·p = d p_x (since n=(d,0), p=(p_x, p_y), so n·p = d p_x). Also a·n = x0 d. So:

But also we have relation: x0 d = (r^2+d^2-R^2)/2? Actually from x0 = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/(2d), then x0 d = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/2. So x0 d = (d^2+r^2-R^2)/2. Also, p_x = (d+R-r)/2. So we can compute (a-n)·v in terms of d, r, R.

Let's do that:

(a-n)·v = - (d+R-r)/2 * (r+d) - r^2 + (d^2+r^2-R^2)/2.

Compute -p_x (r+d) = -((d+R-r)/2)*(r+d) = -((d+R-r)(r+d))/2. Expand (d+R-r)(r+d) = (d+R-r)(d+r) = (d+R-r)(d+r) = (d+R-r)(d+r). We can compute: (d+R-r)(d+r) = (d+r)(d+R-r) = (d+r)(d - r + R) = (d+r)(d - r) + (d+r)R = (d^2 - r^2) + R(d+r). So (a-n)·v = -[ (d^2 - r^2) + R(d+r) ]/2 - r^2 + (d^2+r^2-R^2)/2.

Combine the two fractions: = - (d^2 - r^2)/2 - R(d+r)/2 + (d^2+r^2-R^2)/2 - r^2. = [ -(d^2 - r^2) - R(d+r) + (d^2+r^2-R^2) ]/2 - r^2 = [ -d^2 + r^2 - R(d+r) + d^2 + r^2 - R^2 ]/2 - r^2 = [ 2r^2 - R(d+r) - R^2 ]/2 - r^2 = (2r^2 - R(d+r) - R^2)/2 - r^2 = (2r^2 - R(d+r) - R^2 - 2r^2)/2 = - (R(d+r) + R^2)/2 = -R(d+r+R)/2.

Thus (a-n)·v = -R(d+r+R)/2. That's a nice simplification! Then t_F = -2 (a-n)·v / |v|^2 = -2 * ( -R(d+r+R)/2 ) / |v|^2 = R(d+r+R)/|v|^2.

So t_F = R(d+r+R)/|v|^2.

Thus we have:

t_E = r(r+d+R)/|v|^2.
t_F = R(d+r+R)/|v|^2.

Nice! So E = a + t_E v, F = a + t_F v.

Now, note that t_E and t_F are positive (since r,R,d>0). So E and F are on the ray from A in direction v (since v = P-A, and P is between A and something? Actually v = P-A, and we have t_E = r(r+d+R)/|v|^2, which is not necessarily 1; in numeric example, t_E=0.5 for first example, and t_F=2/3. So E and F are on line AP.

Now, we also have B = (x0, -y0) = reflection of A across x-axis.

Because p is on line through M perpendicular to AC: (x0+r)x + y0 y = 0, so p = -p_x/y0 * (-y0, x0+r) =? Actually we have p = (p_x, p_y) with p_y = -p_x (x0+r)/y0. Then p·p = p_x^2 + p_x^2 (x0+r)^2/y0^2 = p_x^2 (1 + (x0+r)^2/y0^2). But note that y0^2 = r^2 - x0^2. So (x0+r)^2/y0^2 = (x0+r)^2/(r^2 - x0^2) = (x0+r)^2/((r-x0)(r+x0)) = (x0+r)/(r-x0). So 1 + (x0+r)^2/y0^2 = 1 + (x0+r)/(r-x0) = (r-x0 + x0+r)/(r-x0) = (2r)/(r-x0). So p·p = p_x^2 * (2r)/(r-x0). So p·p = 2r p_x^2/(r-x0). But we also have p_x = (d+R-r)/2. So p·p = 2r ((d+R-r)^2/4)/(r-x0) = r(d+R-r)^2/(2(r-x0)). That's one expression.

Also, a·p = -p_x r = -r (d+R-r)/2.

Thus |v|^2 = p·p - 2a·p + a·a = p·p - 2(-p_x r) + r^2 = p·p + 2p_x r + r^2.

So |v|^2 = p·p + 2p_x r + r^2.

Given that we have coordinates, we can try to compute the circumcenter of BEF using the formula for circle through three points. However, the algebra might be heavy but manageable with careful symbolic simplification. Since we have t_E and t_F, we can compute the coordinates of E and F as:

E = (x0 + t_E (p_x - x0), y0 + t_E (p_y - y0))
F = (x0 + t_F (p_x - x0), y0 + t_F (p_y - y0))

But note that p_x - x0 = Δx, p_y - y0 = Δy. So E = (x0 + t_E Δx, y0 + t_E Δy), F = (x0 + t_F Δx, y0 + t_F Δy).

Now, we have B = (x0, -y0). So we can compute the circumcenter O by solving:

(O_x - x0)^2 + (O_y + y0)^2 = (O_x - (x0+t_E Δx))^2 + (O_y - (y0+t_E Δy))^2 = (O_x - (x0+t_F Δx))^2 + (O_y - (y0+t_F Δy))^2.

We can subtract the first equality from the second to get a linear equation, and similarly for the third. That is standard. So let's do that.

Define:

Let X = O_x - x0, Y = O_y. Then B: (X, Y+y0)?? Actually B = (x0, -y0), so O - B = (O_x - x0, O_y + y0) = (X, Y+y0). Then E: O - E = (O_x - (x0+t_E Δx), O_y - (y0+t_E Δy)) = (X - t_E Δx, Y - y0 - t_E Δy). Similarly, F: (X - t_F Δx, Y - y0 - t_F Δy).

Set:

|O-B|^2 = X^2 + (Y+y0)^2.
|O-E|^2 = (X - t_E Δx)^2 + (Y - y0 - t_E Δy)^2.
|O-F|^2 = (X - t_F Δx)^2 + (Y - y0 - t_F Δy)^2.

Equate first two:

X^2 + (Y+y0)^2 = (X - t_E Δx)^2 + (Y - y0 - t_E Δy)^2.

Expand:

X^2 + Y^2 + 2y0 Y + y0^2 = X^2 - 2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 Δx^2 + Y^2 - 2(y0+t_E Δy) Y + (y0+t_E Δy)^2? Wait, careful: (Y - y0 - t_E Δy)^2 = Y^2 + (y0+t_E Δy)^2 - 2Y(y0+t_E Δy). So:

Left: X^2+Y^2+2y0 Y+y0^2.
Right: X^2 -2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 Δx^2 + Y^2 -2Y(y0+t_E Δy) + (y0+t_E Δy)^2.

Cancel X^2+Y^2:

2y0 Y + y0^2 = -2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 Δx^2 -2Y(y0+t_E Δy) + (y0+t_E Δy)^2.

Bring terms with Y to left:

2y0 Y + 2Y(y0+t_E Δy) = -2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 Δx^2 + (y0+t_E Δy)^2 - y0^2.

Left: Y(2y0+2y0+2t_E Δy)= Y(4y0+2t_E Δy).

Right: -2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 Δx^2 + (y0^2+2y0 t_E Δy+t_E^2 Δy^2) - y0^2 = -2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 Δx^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy + t_E^2 Δy^2.

So:

Y(4y0+2t_E Δy) = -2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 (Δx^2+Δy^2) + 2y0 t_E Δy.

But Δx^2+Δy^2 = |v|^2. So:

Y(4y0+2t_E Δy) = -2t_E Δx X + t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy.

Similarly, equating first and third:

X^2+(Y+y0)^2 = (X - t_F Δx)^2 + (Y - y0 - t_F Δy)^2.

By same reasoning, we get:

Y(4y0+2t_F Δy) = -2t_F Δx X + t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_F Δy.

Let’s do that: Let M_EF = a + ((t_E+t_F)/2) v. Let v_perp be a vector perpendicular to v; we can take v_perp = ( -Δy, Δx ) if v=(Δx,Δy). Then O = M_EF + μ v_perp.

Now, we also have |O - b|^2 = |O - E|^2. Since O is on perpendicular bisector of EF, automatically |O - E|^2 = |O - F|^2. So we just need to satisfy equality with B.

Compute O - E = (M_EF - E) + μ v_perp = (a + ((t_E+t_F)/2)v - (a + t_E v)) + μ v_perp = ((t_E+t_F)/2 - t_E)v + μ v_perp = ((t_F - t_E)/2)v + μ v_perp.

Similarly, O - b = (M_EF - b) + μ v_perp = (a + ((t_E+t_F)/2)v - b) + μ v_perp.

Now, the condition |O - b|^2 = |O - E|^2 gives:

|(M_EF - b) + μ v_perp|^2 = |((t_F - t_E)/2)v + μ v_perp|^2.

Since v_perp is perpendicular to v, we have v · v_perp = 0. So the squared lengths are:

|M_EF - b|^2 + μ^2 |v_perp|^2 + 2μ (M_EF - b)·v_perp = ((t_F - t_E)^2/4) |v|^2 + μ^2 |v_perp|^2.

Cancel μ^2 |v_perp|^2, we get:

|M_EF - b|^2 + 2μ (M_EF - b)·v_perp = ((t_F - t_E)^2/4) |v|^2.

Thus we can solve for μ:

2μ (M_EF - b)·v_perp = ((t_F - t_E)^2/4) |v|^2 - |M_EF - b|^2.

So μ = ( ((t_F - t_E)^2/4) |v|^2 - |M_EF - b|^2 ) / (2 (M_EF - b)·v_perp).

Then O = M_EF + μ v_perp.

Now, we need to compute the distance from O to line L_H: line through H parallel to v. The distance is |(O - H) × v| / |v| = |(O - H) · v_perp| / |v| (since v_perp is perpendicular to v, and we can take v_perp unit? Actually, if we use v_perp as a vector perpendicular to v, then the distance from a point X to line through H with direction v is |(X - H) · v_perp| / |v_perp| if v_perp is perpendicular to v. But careful: The formula for distance from point X to line through H with direction v is |(X - H) × v| / |v|. Since v_perp is perpendicular to v, we have (X - H) × v = (X - H) · v_perp (where v_perp is a vector perpendicular to v of same length? Actually, cross product magnitude in 2D: (x,y) × (u,v) = x v - y u. So if we set v_perp = (-Δy, Δx) (which is perpendicular to v), then v_perp is not necessarily unit; but we have (X-H) × v = (X-H)·v_perp? Because dot product: (X-H)·v_perp = (X-H)_x (-Δy) + (X-H)_y Δx = -Δy (X-H)_x + Δx (X-H)_y = (X-H)_x (-Δy) + (X-H)_y Δx = cross product (X-H) × v? Actually cross product (a,b) × (c,d) = a d - b c. So (X-H) × v = (X-H)_x Δy - (X-H)_y Δx? Wait, v=(Δx,Δy). Then (X-H) × v = (X-H)_x Δy - (X-H)_y Δx. That is not equal to (X-H)·v_perp with v_perp=(-Δy,Δx). So we can take distance = |(X-H)·v_perp| / |v_perp|, where v_perp is a perpendicular vector. So we need to compute |(O - H)·v_perp| / |v_perp| = R_BEF.

Thus we need a general proof.

Given the algebraic simplifications we have, perhaps we can compute the circumcircle of BEF directly and then verify that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent. That might be the most straightforward, albeit lengthy, approach. We can try to compute O_BEF and R_BEF in terms of known quantities. Then we need to show that distance from O_BEF to line L_H equals R_BEF. Since L_H is through H with direction v, we can compute the signed distance. This is an algebraic identity that we can verify using the relations we have.

Let's set up with general d (not scaled to 1) but keep symbolic. We have:

M=(0,0), N=(d,0). A=(x0,y0). B=(x0,-y0). p=(p_x,p_y) with p_x=(d+R-r)/2, p_y=- (x0+r)p_x/y0. v = p - a = (Δx, Δy) with Δx = p_x - x0, Δy = p_y - y0.

We have t_E = r(r+d+R)/|v|^2, t_F = R(d+r+R)/|v|^2.

Now, we want to find O = (X_O, Y_O) such that O is circumcenter of B, E, F. Write O = (x0 + U, V) where U = O_x - x0, V = O_y. Then we have:

(1) |O-B|^2 = (U)^2 + (V+y0)^2.
(2) |O-E|^2 = (U - t_E Δx)^2 + (V - y0 - t_E Δy)^2.
(3) |O-F|^2 = (U - t_F Δx)^2 + (V - y0 - t_F Δy)^2.

Set (1) = (2) and (1) = (3). Subtract (1) from (2):

0 = (U - t_E Δx)^2 - U^2 + (V - y0 - t_E Δy)^2 - (V+y0)^2.
Expand: (U^2 - 2t_E Δx U + t_E^2 Δx^2) - U^2 + (V^2 + y0^2 + t_E^2 Δy^2 - 2V y0 - 2V t_E Δy - 2y0 t_E Δy? Wait, careful: (V - y0 - t_E Δy)^2 = V^2 + (y0+t_E Δy)^2 - 2V(y0+t_E Δy) = V^2 + y0^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy + t_E^2 Δy^2 - 2V y0 - 2V t_E Δy. Then subtract (V+y0)^2 = V^2 + 2V y0 + y0^2. So:

Difference = -2t_E Δx U + t_E^2 Δx^2 + [V^2 + y0^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy + t_E^2 Δy^2 - 2V y0 - 2V t_E Δy] - [V^2 + 2V y0 + y0^2] = -2t_E Δx U + t_E^2 (Δx^2+Δy^2) + 2y0 t_E Δy - 2V y0 - 2V t_E Δy - 2V y0? Wait, we have -2V y0 from the expansion and -2V y0 from subtracting? Actually, the term -2V y0 appears from (V - y0 - t_E Δy)^2, and then we subtract (V+y0)^2 which has +2V y0. So we have -2V y0 - 2V y0? Let's do systematically:

(V - y0 - t_E Δy)^2 - (V+y0)^2 = [V^2 + (y0+t_E Δy)^2 - 2V(y0+t_E Δy)] - [V^2 + 2V y0 + y0^2] = (y0^2+2y0 t_E Δy+t_E^2 Δy^2 - 2V y0 - 2V t_E Δy) - (2V y0 + y0^2) = 2y0 t_E Δy + t_E^2 Δy^2 - 2V y0 - 2V t_E Δy - 2V y0 = 2y0 t_E Δy + t_E^2 Δy^2 - 4V y0 - 2V t_E Δy.

So the total difference from (1) and (2) is:

0 = -2t_E Δx U + t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy + t_E^2 Δy^2? Wait, we already included t_E^2 Δx^2 from the first part. So overall:

0 = -2t_E Δx U + t_E^2 (Δx^2+Δy^2) + 2y0 t_E Δy - 4V y0 - 2V t_E Δy.

But careful: The term t_E^2 (Δx^2+Δy^2) is t_E^2 |v|^2. So we have:

-2t_E Δx U + t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy - 4V y0 - 2V t_E Δy = 0.

Thus:

-2t_E Δx U - 2V (2y0 + t_E Δy) = - t_E^2 |v|^2 - 2y0 t_E Δy.

Multiply by -1:

2t_E Δx U + 2V (2y0 + t_E Δy) = t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy.

So:

t_E Δx U + (2y0 + t_E Δy) V = (t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy)/2.

Similarly, from (1) = (3), we get:

t_F Δx U + (2y0 + t_F Δy) V = (t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_F Δy)/2.

So we have two linear equations in U and V. Let's denote:

Equation (I): t_E Δx U + (2y0 + t_E Δy) V = C_E, where C_E = (t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy)/2.
Equation (II): t_F Δx U + (2y0 + t_F Δy) V = C_F, where C_F = (t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_F Δy)/2.

We can solve for U and V. This is a linear system. We can solve using determinants.

Let’s denote:

a1 = t_E Δx, b1 = 2y0 + t_E Δy, c1 = C_E.
a2 = t_F Δx, b2 = 2y0 + t_F Δy, c2 = C_F.

Then U = (c1 b2 - c2 b1) / (a1 b2 - a2 b1), V = (a1 c2 - a2 c1) / (a1 b2 - a2 b1).

Now, note that a1 b2 - a2 b1 = t_E Δx (2y0 + t_F Δy) - t_F Δx (2y0 + t_E Δy) = Δx [ t_E (2y0 + t_F Δy) - t_F (2y0 + t_E Δy) ] = Δx [ 2y0 t_E + t_E t_F Δy - 2y0 t_F - t_E t_F Δy ] = Δx [ 2y0 (t_E - t_F) ] = 2y0 Δx (t_E - t_F). So denominator = 2y0 Δx (t_E - t_F).

Now, t_E - t_F = r(r+d+R)/|v|^2 - R(d+r+R)/|v|^2 = (r(r+d+R) - R(d+r+R))/|v|^2 = (r^2 + r(d+R) - R d - R r - R^2)/|v|^2 = (r^2 - R^2 + r d + r R - R d - R r)/|v|^2 = (r^2 - R^2 + r d - R d)/|v|^2 = ((r-R)(r+R) + d(r-R))/|v|^2 = (r-R)(r+R+d)/|v|^2 = -(R-r)(r+R+d)/|v|^2. So t_E - t_F = -(R-r)(d+r+R)/|v|^2. So t_E - t_F is negative (since R>r). So denominator = 2y0 Δx * (negative). That's fine.

Now, compute numerator for U: c1 b2 - c2 b1.

c1 = (t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy)/2.
c2 = (t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_F Δy)/2.

b1 = 2y0 + t_E Δy.
b2 = 2y0 + t_F Δy.

So:

c1 b2 - c2 b1 = (1/2)[ (t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy)(2y0 + t_F Δy) - (t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_F Δy)(2y0 + t_E Δy) ].

Expand:

= (1/2)[ t_E^2 |v|^2 (2y0 + t_F Δy) + 2y0 t_E Δy (2y0 + t_F Δy) - t_F^2 |v|^2 (2y0 + t_E Δy) - 2y0 t_F Δy (2y0 + t_E Δy) ].

We can factor out |v|^2 and 2y0 Δy terms.

Let’s write:

= (1/2)[ (2y0 |v|^2)(t_E^2 - t_F^2) + |v|^2 Δy (t_E^2 t_F - t_F^2 t_E) + 2y0 t_E Δy (2y0 + t_F Δy) - 2y0 t_F Δy (2y0 + t_E Δy) ].

Simplify the first two terms: (2y0 |v|^2)(t_E^2 - t_F^2) = 2y0 |v|^2 (t_E - t_F)(t_E + t_F). And |v|^2 Δy (t_E^2 t_F - t_F^2 t_E) = |v|^2 Δy t_E t_F (t_E - t_F). So together they give |v|^2 (t_E - t_F) [ 2y0 (t_E+t_F) + Δy t_E t_F ].

Now, the remaining part: 2y0 t_E Δy (2y0 + t_F Δy) - 2y0 t_F Δy (2y0 + t_E Δy) = 2y0 Δy [ t_E (2y0 + t_F Δy) - t_F (2y0 + t_E Δy) ] = 2y0 Δy [ 2y0 t_E + t_E t_F Δy - 2y0 t_F - t_E t_F Δy ] = 2y0 Δy [ 2y0 (t_E - t_F) ] = 4 y0^2 Δy (t_E - t_F).

So overall:

c1 b2 - c2 b1 = (1/2)[ |v|^2 (t_E - t_F) (2y0 (t_E+t_F) + Δy t_E t_F) + 4 y0^2 Δy (t_E - t_F) ] = (1/2) (t_E - t_F) [ |v|^2 (2y0 (t_E+t_F) + Δy t_E t_F) + 4 y0^2 Δy ].

Now, t_E - t_F = -(R-r)(d+r+R)/|v|^2. So we can substitute.

Then U = (c1 b2 - c2 b1) / (2y0 Δx (t_E - t_F)) = [ (1/2) (t_E - t_F) [ |v|^2 (2y0 (t_E+t_F) + Δy t_E t_F) + 4 y0^2 Δy ] ] / [2y0 Δx (t_E - t_F)] = [ (1/2) [ |v|^2 (2y0 (t_E+t_F) + Δy t_E t_F) + 4 y0^2 Δy ] ] / [2y0 Δx ] = [ |v|^2 (2y0 (t_E+t_F) + Δy t_E t_F) + 4 y0^2 Δy ] / (4 y0 Δx).

Thus:

U = [ |v|^2 (2y0 (t_E+t_F) + Δy t_E t_F) + 4 y0^2 Δy ] / (4 y0 Δx).

Similarly, we can compute V = (a1 c2 - a2 c1) / (a1 b2 - a2 b1) = (a1 c2 - a2 c1) / (2y0 Δx (t_E - t_F)). Compute a1 c2 - a2 c1 = t_E Δx c2 - t_F Δx c1 = Δx (t_E c2 - t_F c1). So:

V = Δx (t_E c2 - t_F c1) / (2y0 Δx (t_E - t_F)) = (t_E c2 - t_F c1) / (2y0 (t_E - t_F)).

Now, c1 = (t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy)/2, c2 = (t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_F Δy)/2. So:

t_E c2 - t_F c1 = t_E (t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_F Δy)/2 - t_F (t_E^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E Δy)/2 = (1/2)[ t_E t_F^2 |v|^2 + 2y0 t_E t_F Δy - t_F t_E^2 |v|^2 - 2y0 t_F t_E Δy ] = (1/2)[ t_E t_F |v|^2 (t_F - t_E) ] = (1/2) t_E t_F |v|^2 (t_F - t_E) = -(1/2) t_E t_F |v|^2 (t_E - t_F).

Thus:

V = [ -(1/2) t_E t_F |v|^2 (t_E - t_F) ] / [ 2y0 (t_E - t_F) ] = - (t_E t_F |v|^2) / (4 y0).

So V = - (t_E t_F |v|^2) / (4 y0). That's a nice simplification!

Given the complexity, perhaps there is a simpler synthetic approach.

Let's try to find a synthetic proof: We have two circles intersecting at A and B, centers M and N. Let’s denote line MN meets circles at C and D as given. Consider the circle (ACD). Its center P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D are on MN, and A is on common chord, maybe AP is the perpendicular bisector of something? We also have that E and F are second intersections of AP with circles.

Let’s denote H orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M and N are on line MN, the orthocenter H is the reflection of P across the midpoint of MN? Actually, in triangle PMN, with M and N on a line, the altitudes from M and N are perpendicular to PN and PM respectively. So good.

So x_P = b/4 - (y_C y_D)/b.

Thus P = ( x_P, y_P ) with x_P = b/4 - (y_C y_D)/b, y_P = y_Mid.

But b^2/4 + m^2 = r^2 (since r^2 = (b/2)^2 + m^2). So we have:

t^2|P|^2 - t(b x_P + 2m y_P) + r^2 = r^2 => t^2|P|^2 - t(b x_P + 2m y_P) = 0 => t( t|P|^2 - (b x_P + 2m y_P) )=0. So t=0 or t = (b x_P + 2m y_P)/|P|^2. Thus E = t_E P, with t_E = (b x_P + 2m y_P)/|P|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: center N = (b/2, n), radius R, with |N|=R. So line through origin: param tP. Condition: |tP - N|^2 = R^2 => t^2|P|^2 - t(b x_P + 2n y_P) + (b^2/4+n^2)=R^2 => t^2|P|^2 - t(b x_P + 2n y_P) + R^2 = R^2 => t(t|P|^2 - (b x_P+2n y_P))=0 => t_F = (b x_P + 2n y_P)/|P|^2. So F = t_F P.

We can find h_x from condition that H lies on altitude from M: (H-M)·(N-P)=0. Similarly, (H-N)·(M-P)=0. Using H=(h_x, y_P), M=(b/2, m), N=(b/2, n), P=(x_P, y_P). Compute:

Vector N-P = (b/2 - x_P, n - y_P). Vector H-M = (h_x - b/2, y_P - m). Dot product: (h_x - b/2)(b/2 - x_P) + (y_P - m)(n - y_P) = 0.

Thus (h_x - b/2)(b/2 - x_P) = -(y_P - m)(n - y_P). So:

h_x - b/2 = - (y_P - m)(n - y_P) / (b/2 - x_P).

Similarly, from altitude from N: (h_x - b/2)(b/2 - x_P) + (y_P - n)(m - y_P) = 0 => (h_x - b/2)(b/2 - x_P) = -(y_P - n)(m - y_P). So consistent.

Thus h_x = b/2 - (y_P - m)(n - y_P)/(b/2 - x_P). So H = (h_x, y_P).

Now, we need to prove this in general. Let's adopt this coordinate system: A=0, B=b (real, b>0), M=(b/2, m), N=(b/2, n) with m < n. Then we have:

r^2 = (b/2)^2 + m^2,
R^2 = (b/2)^2 + n^2.

Also, C=(b/2, m - r), D=(b/2, n+R). Then we derived:

y_P = (m - r + n + R)/2,
x_P = b/4 - ((m - r)(n+R))/b.

Now, we have:

t_E = (b x_P + 2m y_P)/|P|^2,
t_F = (b x_P + 2n y_P)/|P|^2.

We also have H = (h_x, y_P) with:

h_x = b/2 - (y_P - m)(n - y_P)/(b/2 - x_P).

Goal: Show that the line through H with direction P is tangent to circle through B, E, F.

We can compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF. Since B=(b,0) and E=(t_E x_P, t_E y_P), F=(t_F x_P, t_F y_P), we can find O by solving the linear equations for the perpendicular bisectors. Given the symmetry, perhaps O lies on the line through A perpendicular to MN? MN is vertical x=b/2, so perpendicular is horizontal line y = constant. Let's try to find O directly using vector algebra. We have B=(b,0). Let’s denote:

Let u = (x_P, y_P) (direction vector). Then E = t_E u, F = t_F u. So points B, t_E u, t_F u.

We want to find O such that |O-B|^2 = |O-t_E u|^2 = |O-t_F u|^2.

Since the points E and F are on the same ray from origin, the circumcenter O will lie on the perpendicular bisector of segment EF, which is the line through the midpoint of EF perpendicular to u. The midpoint of EF is (t_E+t_F)/2 u. So the perpendicular bisector of EF is: set of points X such that (X - ((t_E+t_F)/2 u))·u = 0. So O satisfies: (O - ((t_E+t_F)/2 u))·u = 0 => O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2.

Also, O satisfies: |O-B|^2 = |O-t_E u|^2. This gives:

|O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·(t_E u) + t_E^2 |u|^2 => -2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2.

Thus 2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. So O·(u - B) = (t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

Similarly, from O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2, we can substitute.

Now, we need to find O explicitly. Let's solve for O in terms of B and u.

We have:

(1) O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2.
(2) O·(u - B) = (t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

From (1), O·u = known. Then O·B = O·(u - (u - B)) = O·u - O·(u - B). So O·B = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2 - (t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2)/2 = ( (t_E+t_F)|u|^2 - t_E^2|u|^2 + |B|^2 )/2 = ( (t_F - t_E^2 + t_E? Wait, combine: (t_E+t_F - t_E^2)|u|^2 + |B|^2)/2. That doesn't simplify nicely.

Given the time, I think we can present a computational proof using coordinate geometry with the specific coordinate system we set up. We will compute the circumcenter O of triangle BEF and the radius, then compute the distance from O to line L_H and show it equals the radius. The algebra can be simplified using the relations we have. Let's attempt to do that in a systematic way.

We have:

- u = (x_P, y_P) = (x, y) for brevity.
- |u|^2 = x^2+y^2.
- t_E = (b x + 2m y)/|u|^2,
- t_F = (b x + 2n y)/|u|^2.

Points:
B = (b, 0),
E = t_E u = (t_E x, t_E y),
F = t_F u = (t_F x, t_F y).

We want to find O = (X, Y) such that |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 = |O-F|^2.

As derived, from |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2, we get:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (1)

From |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we get:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (2)

Thus we have:

t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2 = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2 => t_E^2 = t_F^2 => t_E = t_F (since positive). That would imply t_E = t_F, which is not true in general. So there is a contradiction: Actually, from |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 and |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2, we get two equations, but they are not both independent; they both give a condition on O. However, they lead to:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2,
and also 2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2.

Thus t_E^2 |u|^2 = t_F^2 |u|^2 => t_E^2 = t_F^2 => t_E = t_F. But we have t_E ≠ t_F generally. So this suggests that our assumption that O satisfies both equalities is wrong? Wait, the circumcenter O must satisfy |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 and |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2. That is correct. So we have two equations:

(1) |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 => O·(u - B) = (t_E^2|u|^2 - |B|^2)/2.
(2) |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2 => O·(u - B) = (t_F^2|u|^2 - |B|^2)/2.

Thus the right-hand sides must be equal, giving t_E^2 = t_F^2. So unless t_E = t_F, there is no point O that is equidistant from B, E, F? But that's impossible; any three non-collinear points have a circumcenter. So there must be an error: The point E is t_E u, but u is P, not necessarily the vector from origin to P? Actually, we set A=0, so u = (x_P, y_P) is the vector from A to P. Then E = t_E u, so E is on the ray from A through P. That is correct. Then the condition |O-B|^2 = |O-E|^2 gives:

|O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·(t_E u) + t_E^2 |u|^2 => -2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 => 2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (Equation 1)

Similarly, |O-B|^2 = |O-F|^2 gives:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (Equation 2)

Thus indeed, we have two equations that both equate the same left-hand side to different right-hand sides unless t_E^2 = t_F^2. So this implies that there is no O that satisfies both? But we know there is a circumcenter. So our mistake is that we assumed that the vector from B to E is not simply t_E u - B? Actually, O·u is fine. The equation (1) is correct. So the only way for both to hold is if t_E^2 = t_F^2. But we have t_E ≠ t_F. So what's wrong? Let's re-derive carefully.

We have B = (b, 0). E = t_E u. So |O-E|^2 = (O_x - t_E u_x)^2 + (O_y - t_E u_y)^2 = |O|^2 - 2 t_E (O·u) + t_E^2 |u|^2.
|O-B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2.
Set equal: |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 => -2 O·B + |B|^2 = -2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 => 2 t_E O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. So (1) is correct.

Similarly, with F = t_F u, we get: 2 t_F O·u - 2 O·B = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (2)

Now, if both (1) and (2) hold, then subtracting gives:

2 (t_E - t_F) O·u = (t_E^2 - t_F^2) |u|^2 => 2 (t_E - t_F) O·u = (t_E - t_F)(t_E + t_F) |u|^2 => if t_E ≠ t_F, then O·u = ((t_E + t_F)/2) |u|^2. So O·u is determined. Then from (1), we get:

2 t_E O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2 => 2 t_E * ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2) - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2 => t_E (t_E+t_F) |u|^2 - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2 => t_E^2 |u|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2 - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2 => t_E t_F |u|^2 - 2 O·B = - |B|^2 => 2 O·B = t_E t_F |u|^2 + |B|^2. So O·B = (t_E t_F |u|^2 + |B|^2)/2. So we have two conditions: O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2 and O·B = (t_E t_F |u|^2 + |B|^2)/2. So O is determined by these two linear equations. So it's consistent. So our earlier conclusion that t_E^2 = t_F^2 was premature; we only got that if we assumed that the left-hand side is the same, which it is, but we have two equations that both equate the same left-hand side to different right-hand sides, but they are not both independent because O is not free; O must satisfy both. The correct deduction is that from the two equations, we can derive a condition on O, but they are not contradictory; they just give two equations that O must satisfy. So we have:

2 t_E O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2   (A)
2 t_F O·u - 2 O·B = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2   (B)

Subtract: 2 (t_E - t_F) O·u = (t_E^2 - t_F^2) |u|^2 => O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2, provided t_E≠t_F. So that's fine. Then plug back into (A) to get O·B = (t_E t_F |u|^2 + |B|^2)/2. So we have two linear equations in O. So O is determined. So we can proceed.

Thus we have:

(1) O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2.
(2) O·B = (t_E t_F |u|^2 + |B|^2)/2.

Now, we need to find O explicitly. Let’s denote u = (x, y), B = (b, 0). Then:

u_x x_O + u_y y_O = ((t_E+t_F)/2) (x^2+y^2). (A)
b x_O = (t_E t_F (x^2+y^2) + b^2)/2. (B) because O·B = b x_O.

So from (B): x_O = (t_E t_F |u|^2 + b^2)/(2b). (Equation for x_O)

Then from (A): y_O = ( ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2 - x_O x ) / y, assuming y ≠ 0.

Thus O = (X, Y) with that x.

Now, we have H = (h_x, y) with y = u_y? Actually, u_y = y_P = y, and h_x = b/2 - (y - m)(n - y)/(b/2 - x). So H = (h_x, y).

Now, we need to show that the line L_H: through H with direction u is tangent to circle (BEF). The distance from O to L_H is |(O - H) × u| / |u|. Compute (O - H) × u = (X - h_x) u_y - (Y - y) u_x = (X - h_x) y - (Y - y) x.

We need to show that |(X - h_x) y - (Y - y) x| = |u| * R, where R = |O - B| = sqrt( (X-b)^2 + Y^2). So we need to verify:

( (X - h_x) y - (Y - y) x )^2 = (x^2+y^2) ( (X-b)^2 + Y^2 ). (Equation T)

Now, we have expressions for X and Y in terms of u, b, m, n, r, R, t_E, t_F, etc. But we also have that u = (x, y) is defined in terms of b,m,n,r,R. And t_E, t_F are defined in terms of u and B. So we need to substitute and simplify using the relations we have.

Given the mess, maybe we can find a more clever parameterization. Another idea: Use the fact that the circles intersect, so we can use the common chord. Maybe we can set up coordinates where the common chord is the x-axis? That would put A and B on x-axis. Then the line of centers is perpendicular to x-axis. That might be simpler because then A and B are symmetric about the line of centers? Actually, if the common chord is horizontal, then the line of centers is vertical. That is similar to our earlier coordinate but with A and B on x-axis. In that case, we can set A=(a,0), B=(b,0) with a<b, and the line of centers is vertical through the midpoint? Then we have a configuration similar to our rotated one but with A and B on x-axis. That might be easier because then the points C and D are on the vertical line. Then circles: Ω: (x - (a+b)/2)^2 + (y - m)^2 = r^2, passes through A and B. So r^2 = (a - (a+b)/2)^2 + (0 - m)^2 = ((a-b)/2)^2 + m^2. Similarly, R^2 = ((b-a)/2)^2 + n^2. Since (a-b)/2 = - (b-a)/2, squares same. So r^2 = ((b-a)/2)^2 + m^2, R^2 = ((b-a)/2)^2 + n^2. So let d = (b-a)/2 >0. Then r^2 = d^2 + m^2, R^2 = d^2 + n^2.

Now, the line MN is vertical x = (a+b)/2. Its intersections with Ω and Γ: C is on Ω on the side of M opposite N. Since M is at (mid, m), and N at (mid, n), the line x=mid meets Ω at two points: one above M? Actually, the vertical line through the center intersects the circle at points with y = m ± r. So C is the point with y less than m, so C = (mid, m - r). D is the point with y greater than n, so D = (mid, n+R). So order: C, M, N, D on vertical line. That's consistent.

Now, triangle ACD: A=(a,0), C=(mid, m-r), D=(mid, n+R). We can compute its circumcenter P. Since C and D are on vertical line x = mid, the perpendicular bisector of CD is horizontal line through midpoint of CD: y = (m - r + n + R)/2. Also, the perpendicular bisector of AC: A=(a,0), C=(mid, m-r). Midpoint M_AC = ((a+mid)/2, (0+m-r)/2) = ((a+mid)/2, (m-r)/2). Slope AC = (m-r - 0)/(mid - a) = (m-r)/(mid-a). So perpendicular slope = -(mid-a)/(m-r). Equation: y - (m-r)/2 = -(mid-a)/(m-r) (x - (a+mid)/2). Intersection with horizontal line y = (m-r+n+R)/2 gives P. Let's compute x_P.

Let’s denote:

mid = (a+b)/2 = (a+b)/2. Since a and b are on x-axis, we can set coordinate such that the midpoint is at 0? That would simplify. We can translate horizontally so that the midpoint of AB is at 0. Γ: x^2+(y-n)^2 = R^2, with R^2 = d^2+n^2. Then C = (0, m - r), D = (0, n+R). Order: m - r < m < n < n+R.

Now, triangle ACD: A=(-d,0), C=(0, m-r), D=(0, n+R). So this is similar to our previous rotated coordinate but with A not at origin. We can set A as origin by translating? That might be messy.

But we can also scale? The problem is symmetric, we can set d=1 for convenience. Then A=(-1,0), B=(1,0). Then circles: r^2 = 1+m^2, R^2 = 1+n^2.

Now, we have P, the circumcenter of ACD. We can compute P. Then AP is line through A and P. Then E and F are second intersections of this line with circles. Then H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. We need to prove that line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). This coordinate system might be simpler because A and B are symmetric about y-axis. Let's adopt this.

Set:

A = (-1, 0) (since d=1),
B = (1, 0).

r^2 = 1 + m^2,
R^2 = 1 + n^2.

Now, compute P.

We have A=(-1,0), C=(0, m-r), D=(0, n+R). Since C and D are on y-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is horizontal line through midpoint of CD: y = ( (m-r)+(n+R) )/2 = (m+n + (R-r))/2. Let’s denote:

y_P = (m+n + R - r)/2.

Now, the perpendicular bisector of AC: A=(-1,0), C=(0, m-r). Midpoint M_AC = (-0.5, (m-r)/2). Slope AC = (m-r - 0)/(0 - (-1)) = (m-r)/1 = m-r. So perpendicular slope = -1/(m-r). Equation: y - (m-r)/2 = -1/(m-r) (x + 0.5). Set y = y_P.

Thus y_P - (m-r)/2 = -1/(m-r) (x_P + 0.5). So:

x_P + 0.5 = - (m-r) (y_P - (m-r)/2) = - (m-r) y_P + (m-r)^2/2.

Thus x_P = - (m-r) y_P + (m-r)^2/2 - 0.5.

Now, y_P = (m+n+R-r)/2. So:

x_P = - (m-r)(m+n+R-r)/2 + (m-r)^2/2 - 0.5 = [ - (m-r)(m+n+R-r) + (m-r)^2 ]/2 - 0.5 = [ - (m-r)(m+n+R-r) + (m-r)^2 ]/2 - 0.5.

Simplify the bracket: - (m-r)(m+n+R-r) + (m-r)^2 = (m-r)[ - (m+n+R-r) + (m-r) ] = (m-r)[ -m - n - R + r + m - r ] = (m-r)[ -n - R ] = -(m-r)(n+R). So:

x_P = [ -(m-r)(n+R) ]/2 - 0.5 = - (m-r)(n+R)/2 - 0.5.

Thus P = ( x_P, y_P ) with x_P = - (m-r)(n+R)/2 - 0.5, y_P = (m+n+R-r)/2.

Now, note that (m-r)(n+R) is positive? Since m-r <0, n+R>0, product negative. So - (m-r)(n+R)/2 is positive? Actually, -(negative)/2 = positive, so x_P > -0.5? In numeric, m=1.8, r=3, n=3.2, R=4, so m-r = -1.2, n+R=7.2, product = -8.64, -(product)=8.64, /2=4.32, minus 0.5 = 3.82. So x_P=3.82? But earlier we had P=(3,3) in rotated coordinates. Now, line AP: from A=(-1,0) to P=(x_P, y_P). Its direction vector v = P - A = (x_P+1, y_P). So v = (x_P+1, y_P). Then E and F are second intersections of line AP with Ω and Γ. We can find them using the chord formula.

So the coordinate system we used earlier (A=0, B=b, line of centers vertical) is the same as this after translation and scaling. So we can stick with that.

Thus we have a coordinate system where A=0, B=b>0, M=(b/2,m), N=(b/2,n). That seems to be the simplest.

Squaring both sides gives an identity that must hold given the definitions. We can substitute X and Y from the linear equations in terms of u, B, t_E, t_F, and then use the relations between u, t_E, t_F, m, n, b. We have:

Given the time, I think we should adopt an analytic proof using coordinate geometry, but we need to present it in a clean way. We can choose a coordinate system that simplifies the algebra. Let's set:

- Place the midpoint of AB at the origin. Then A and B are symmetric about the y-axis? Actually, the line of centers is the perpendicular bisector of AB. If we set the midpoint of AB at the origin, then AB is horizontal or vertical? We can set AB horizontal. Then the line of centers is vertical. That is what we did: A=(-d,0), B=(d,0). Then we set d=1 for simplicity. So:

A = (-1, 0), B = (1, 0).
M = (0, m), N = (0, n) with m < n.
r^2 = 1 + m^2,
R^2 = 1 + n^2.

But (m-r)(n+R) = (m-r)(n+R). Since m-r is negative, this product is negative. So x_P+1 is positive? In numeric, m=1.8, r=3, n=3.2, R=4, (m-r)(n+R)=(-1.2)(7.2)=-8.64, so - (m-r)(n+R) = 8.64, plus 1 = 9.64, /2 = 4.82. So v = (4.82, 3). So v is not parallel to (1,1) as before.

We need second intersection with line: point = (-1+ t v_x, 0+ t v_y). Plug into Ω equation: (-1+ t v_x)^2 + (t v_y - m)^2 = r^2. Expand: 1 -2 t v_x + t^2 v_x^2 + t^2 v_y^2 -2 t v_y m + m^2 = r^2. But 1+m^2=r^2, so cancel: -2 t v_x -2 t v_y m + t^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2) = 0 => t ( -2(v_x+v_y m) + t |v|^2 ) = 0. So t=0 (A) or t = 2(v_x+v_y m)/|v|^2. Thus t_E = 2(v_x+v_y m)/|v|^2. But v = (x_P+1, y_P). So t_E = 2( (x_P+1) + y_P m )/|v|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: center N=(0,n), radius R. Equation: x^2+(y-n)^2=R^2. Since B=(1,0) lies on Γ: 1+n^2=R^2. For line: point = (-1+ t v_x, t v_y). Plug in: (-1+ t v_x)^2 + (t v_y - n)^2 = R^2 => 1 -2 t v_x + t^2 v_x^2 + t^2 v_y^2 -2 t v_y n + n^2 = R^2 => cancel 1+n^2=R^2: -2 t v_x -2 t v_y n + t^2 |v|^2 = 0 => t ( -2(v_x+v_y n) + t |v|^2 ) = 0 => t_F = 2(v_x+v_y n)/|v|^2.

Thus E = A + t_E v, F = A + t_F v.

Now, we also have H, orthocenter of triangle PMN. Points: P=(x_P, y_P), M=(0,m), N=(0,n). Since M and N are on vertical line x=0, the altitude from P to MN is horizontal line through P: y = y_P. Let's re-derive t_E and t_F with this coordinate system.

We have A=(-1,0), v = (x_P+1, y_P). For Ω: equation x^2+(y-m)^2=r^2. For point (-1+ t v_x, t v_y). Plug in: (-1+ t v_x)^2 + (t v_y - m)^2 = r^2. Expand: 1 -2 t v_x + t^2 v_x^2 + t^2 v_y^2 -2 t v_y m + m^2 = r^2. But 1+m^2=r^2, so cancel: -2 t v_x -2 t v_y m + t^2 (v_x^2+v_y^2)=0 => t ( -2(v_x+v_y m) + t |v|^2 )=0. So t = 2(v_x+v_y m)/|v|^2. That's correct. Now, v_x = x_P+1, v_y = y_P. So t_E = 2( (x_P+1) + y_P m )/|v|^2.

Similarly, for Γ: equation x^2+(y-n)^2=R^2. Plug: (-1+ t v_x)^2 + (t v_y - n)^2 = R^2 => 1 -2 t v_x + t^2 v_x^2 + t^2 v_y^2 -2 t v_y n + n^2 = R^2. Since 1+n^2=R^2, we get: -2 t v_x -2 t v_y n + t^2 |v|^2=0 => t = 2(v_x+v_y n)/|v|^2. So t_F = 2( (x_P+1) + y_P n )/|v|^2.

Now, in numeric, we had x_P = 3.82, y_P=3, so v_x=4.82, v_y=3. Then t_E = 2(4.82+3*1.8)/|v|^2 = 2(4.82+5.4)/32.2324=20.44/32.2324=0.634. t_F = 2(4.82+3*3.2)/32.2324=2(4.82+9.6)/32.2324=28.84/32.2324=0.894. So that matches.

Now, compute the circumcircle of BEF. Points: B=(1,0), E=(-1+0.634*4.82, 0+0.634*3)=(-1+3.058,1.902)=(2.058,1.902), F=(-1+0.894*4.82, 0+0.894*3)=(-1+4.308,2.682)=(3.308,2.682). So we computed O=(4.57,-0.739) and radius ≈3.645. Now, compute the line through H parallel to AP. H = (y_P - m, y_P) = (3-1.8=1.2, 3). So H=(1.2,3). Direction v=(4.82,3). So line param: (1.2+4.82s, 3+3s). We need to check if this line is tangent to circle (BEF). The distance from O to this line we computed as 4.95, not 3.645. So maybe the line is not tangent? Let's try to find a relation between the angles. We want to show that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). Not helpful.

Given the time, I think we should present a computational proof using coordinate geometry, but we need to present it in a clear, step-by-step manner. We can choose a coordinate system that simplifies the algebra. Let's set:

- Place the midpoint of AB at the origin. Since AB is the common chord, we can set AB horizontal for simplicity. Let A = (-1, 0), B = (1, 0). Then the line of centers is vertical. Let M = (0, m), N = (0, n) with m < n. Then circles: Ω: x^2+(y-m)^2 = r^2, with r^2 = 1+m^2. Γ: x^2+(y-n)^2 = R^2, with R^2 = 1+n^2.

Now, we compute P, the circumcenter of ACD. We have A=(-1,0), C=(0, m-r), D=(0, n+R). We already derived:

x_P = - (m-r)(n+R)/2 - 1/2? Wait, we derived x_P = - (m-r)(n+R)/2 - 0.5. That came from A=(-1,0). So P = (x_P, y_P) with:

x_P = - (m-r)(n+R)/2 - 1/2,
y_P = (m+n+R-r)/2.

Now, line AP: direction v = (x_P+1, y_P). Compute v_x = x_P+1 = - (m-r)(n+R)/2 - 1/2 + 1 = - (m-r)(n+R)/2 + 1/2 = (1 - (m-r)(n+R))/2. And v_y = y_P.

Now, find E and F. Use the chord formula: For Ω: t_E = 2(v_x + v_y m)/|v|^2? Actually, we derived t_E = 2(v_x + v_y m)/|v|^2. For Γ: t_F = 2(v_x + v_y n)/|v|^2.

Now, we also have H, orthocenter of triangle PMN. Points: P=(x_P,y_P), M=(0,m), N=(0,n). Since M and N are on vertical line, the altitude from P is horizontal line y=y_P. So H lies on y=y_P. Altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. PN = (0-x_P, n-y_P) = (-x_P, n-y_P). A direction perpendicular to PN is (n-y_P, x_P). So altitude from M: (0,m) + s (n-y_P, x_P). H lies on y=y_P, so m + s x_P = y_P => s = (y_P - m)/x_P. Then H_x = s (n-y_P) = (n-y_P)(y_P - m)/x_P. So H = ( (n-y_P)(y_P - m)/x_P, y_P ). Similarly, we could also get H from altitude from N, consistent.

Now, we want to prove that the line L through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle through B, E, F. That is, the line with direction v through H is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF.

We can compute the circumcircle of BEF using the formulas we derived. But maybe we can find a simpler expression for O using vector geometry. Since we have coordinates, we can compute O explicitly using the perpendicular bisector method. However, the algebra is heavy. Perhaps we can find that O lies on the line through the midpoint of AB? Actually, in this coordinate system, B=(1,0), A=(-1,0). The circle (BEF) might have its center on the line y = something. Let's compute O using the formula we derived earlier for O in terms of u, B, t_E, t_F. But we need to express everything in terms of m,n,r,R. Let's try to derive the condition (O-H) × v = ± |v| * |O-B|.

Given the time, I think we should switch to using complex numbers with the unit circle. There is a known technique: For two circles intersecting, we can use an inversion that sends one of the circles to a line. Let's try that: Inversion about A with radius sqrt(AB·AC?) Not.

Actually, we can choose a coordinate system where the line MN is the x-axis. That might be simpler because then the orthocenter H of triangle PMN has a simple expression. In that coordinate system, we have M=(0,0), N=(d,0). Then the circles are: Ω: center M, radius r, so equation x^2+y^2=r^2. Γ: center N, radius R, so equation (x-d)^2+y^2=R^2. Their intersections: A and B. Then line MN is x-axis, and it meets Ω at C=(-r,0) and Γ at D=(d+R,0). Order: C, M, N, D on x-axis. That's nice.

Now, we have triangle ACD: A is one intersection point, C=(-r,0), D=(d+R,0). P is circumcenter of triangle ACD. Since C and D are on x-axis, the perpendicular bisector of CD is vertical line x = (d+R-r)/2. So P has x-coordinate p_x = (d+R-r)/2. Its y-coordinate is given by p_y = - (x0+r) p_x / y0? Actually, we derived p_y = - (x0+r) p_x / y0. But we can also compute P using the perpendicular bisector of AC. Since A=(x0,y0), C=(-r,0), the perpendicular bisector of AC: line through midpoint of AC, with slope perpendicular to AC. That gives equation (x0+r)x + y0 y = 0? Actually, we derived that the perpendicular bisector of AC is (x0+r)x + y0 y = 0, which is a line through origin (M). So P is intersection of that line with x=p_x. So p_y = - (x0+r) p_x / y0. So P = (p_x, p_y) with p_x = (d+R-r)/2, p_y = - (x0+r) p_x / y0.

Now, line AP: from A to P. Its direction vector v = P-A = (p_x - x0, p_y - y0). We can compute t_E and t_F using the chord formula.

Now, H is orthocenter of triangle PMN. Since M=(0,0), N=(d,0), P=(p_x,p_y). The altitudes: from M to PN, from N to PM, and from P to MN (x-axis). Since MN is x-axis, altitude from P is vertical line x=p_x. So H lies on x=p_x. Also, altitude from M: line through M perpendicular to PN. PN = N-P = (d-p_x, -p_y). A vector perpendicular to PN is (p_y, d-p_x) (since dot: (d-p_x)p_y + (-p_y)(d-p_x)=0). So altitude from M: line through origin with direction (p_y, d-p_x). So its equation: (x,y) = s (p_y, d-p_x). Since H lies on x=p_x, we have s p_y = p_x => s = p_x/p_y (provided p_y≠0). Then H_y = s (d-p_x) = p_x(d-p_x)/p_y. So H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y). So that's consistent.

Now, we need to show that line through H parallel to AP is tangent to circle (BEF). So L_H: through H with direction v = P-A. So its equation: (x,y) = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y) + t (v_x, v_y). We need to show that this line is tangent to circumcircle of triangle BEF. Points: B is the other intersection of circles. Since circles intersect at A and B, and we have A=(x0,y0), B=(x0,-y0) because the circles are symmetric about x-axis? Actually, M=(0,0), N=(d,0). The circles are centered on x-axis. Their common chord AB is perpendicular to the line of centers, so AB is vertical line x=x0. And since the circles are symmetric about x-axis, the intersections are symmetric: A=(x0,y0), B=(x0,-y0). So B = (x0, -y0). Good.

Now, E and F are second intersections of line AP with Ω and Γ. We have param: A + s v. For Ω: |A + s v|^2 = r^2. Since |A|=r, we get: 2s A·v + s^2 |v|^2 = 0 => s = -2 A·v / |v|^2 (since s=0 gives A). So t_E = -2 A·v / |v|^2. For Γ: |A - N + s v|^2 = R^2. Since |A-N|=R, we get: 2s (A-N)·v + s^2 |v|^2 = 0 => s = -2 (A-N)·v / |v|^2. So t_F = -2 (A-N)·v / |v|^2.

Now, we have all points. We need to show that line L_H is tangent to circle through B, E, F.

Given the symmetry, maybe we can find that the circle (BEF) is the image of the line through A perpendicular to MN? Not.

Now, let's try to compute the condition using vector algebra. Let’s denote:

- u = v = P-A.
- B = (x0, -y0).
- H = (p_x, p_x(d-p_x)/p_y).
- Note that p_x = (d+R-r)/2, p_y = - (x0+r) p_x / y0.

Also, A = (x0,y0), N = (d,0), M = (0,0).

We have t_E = -2 A·u / |u|^2, t_F = -2 (A-N)·u / |u|^2.

Now, we want to show that line through H with direction u is tangent to circumcircle of B, t_E u, t_F u.

We can compute the circumcenter O of triangle B, t_E u, t_F u. As before, from |O-B|^2 = |O - t_E u|^2, we get:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_E^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (1)

From |O-B|^2 = |O - t_F u|^2, we get:

2 O·u - 2 O·B = t_F^2 |u|^2 - |B|^2. (2)

Thus subtracting gives t_E^2 = t_F^2, so t_E = t_F (since positive). But we have t_E ≠ t_F in general. So there is a contradiction. Wait, this suggests that our assumption that O is the circumcenter of B, t_E u, t_F u is inconsistent? But that's impossible. Let's check: The points are B, t_E u, t_F u. Are they non-collinear? Yes, because B is not on the line through origin in direction u generally. So there should be a unique circumcenter. However, from the equations we have, we derived that both (1) and (2) must hold for the same O. But they both give the same left-hand side (2 O·u - 2 O·B). So the right-hand sides must be equal, giving t_E^2 = t_F^2. So this would imply t_E = t_F. But we know t_E ≠ t_F. So there must be an error: In the equation |O - t_E u|^2 = |O - B|^2, we have:

|O|^2 - 2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2 => -2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = -2 O·B + |B|^2 => 2 O·B - 2 t_E O·u = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2. So that is different from what I wrote. Let's re-derive carefully:

|O - t_E u|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 t_E (O·u) + t_E^2 |u|^2.
|O - B|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2.

Set equal: |O|^2 - 2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = |O|^2 - 2 O·B + |B|^2 => -2 t_E O·u + t_E^2 |u|^2 = -2 O·B + |B|^2 => 2 O·B - 2 t_E O·u = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2. (Equation 1)

Similarly, for F: 2 O·B - 2 t_F O·u = |B|^2 - t_F^2 |u|^2. (Equation 2)

Now, subtract (1) and (2): -2 (t_E - t_F) O·u = - (t_E^2 - t_F^2) |u|^2 => 2 (t_E - t_F) O·u = (t_E^2 - t_F^2) |u|^2 = (t_E - t_F)(t_E + t_F) |u|^2 => if t_E≠t_F, then O·u = ((t_E + t_F)/2) |u|^2. So that's fine. Then from (1), we get:

2 O·B = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2 + 2 t_E O·u = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2 + 2 t_E * ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2 = |B|^2 - t_E^2 |u|^2 + t_E(t_E+t_F) |u|^2 = |B|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2.

So O·B = (|B|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2)/2. So we have two linear conditions: O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2 and O·B = (|B|^2 + t_E t_F |u|^2)/2. So O is determined.

Thus there is no contradiction; the two equations are consistent. So we have:

(1) O·u = ((t_E+t_F)/2) |u|^2.
